            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7bddeff1-a506-4f0f-94b9-12f8374d5403/adfm202414761-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7bddeff1-a506-4f0f-94b9-12f8374d5403/adfm202414761-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a184d503-992b-4b8a-9492-e79c89f8ec64/adfm202414761-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7bddeff1-a506-4f0f-94b9-12f8374d5403/adfm202414761-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological and structural analysis of flexible HCNFs-1.2 film: a) Digital photograph for the evolution from precursor to end-product, b) digital illustrations for flexibility of the film, c,d) FESEM images, e) TEM and f–i) HRTEM images and corresponding intensity profile for measuring interlayer spacings, and j) STEM and corresponding EDS elemental (C, N, and Bi) mapping images.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414761-sec-0040-title">2.2 Formation Mechanism of Flexible HCNFs Films</h3>
            
            <p>To gain more in-depth understanding of formation mechanism of the flexible HCNFs film, systematical parallel experiments were purposefully designed and conducted. First, as for the case without the addition of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, the fibrous morphology cannot be well maintained even after the simple pre-oxidation, and the serious fusion phenomenon occurs between the fibers (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,b</a>, Supporting Information). By contrast, the well-defined fiber shape is successfully maintained with the further introduction of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Besides this, it should be noted that a large number of nanoparticles (NPs) of ≈20&nbsp;nm in diameter appear on the fiber surface, which is related to the generation of bismuth oxide NPs from the decomposition of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O at the pre-oxidation temperature of 230&nbsp;°C. From the STEM and corresponding element mapping images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information), the nano bismuth oxide is evenly distributed in and/or upon the fibers. Moreover, we directly carbonize the fiber film without pre-oxidation, and explore the influence of pre-oxidation step. Apparently, the complete collapse of the fiber structure is clear (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), which should mainly result from the release of more gas during the direct carbonization of PVP and Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, and the high thermal shrinkage rate of the fibers, leading to the structural collapse of nanofibers. Accordingly, the classic pre-oxidation procedure can partially oxidize elements such as hydrogen and nitrogen species in polymer fibers, and form oxides with higher oxygen content, thereby reducing the amount of gas released during fiber carbonization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0028_R_d435183099e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS ES&amp;T Engineering">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, it is easy to draw a undisputed conclusion that the addition of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O and the pre-oxidation process are equally essential to the success of non-melting carbonization of fibers.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, we explored the specific evolution of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O in the polymer fibers with different annealing temperatures by taking HCNFs-1.2 as an example, as collected in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0002">2a</a>. Evidently, within the calcination temperature range from 400 to 800&nbsp;°C, the characteristic X-ray diffraction (XRD) peaks of metallic Bi (JCPDS no. 44–1246) can be easily discerned, and the signals of derivative Bi become even weaker with the increase in temperatures. This should be related to the more serious physical sublimation of the carbothermal reduction induced Bi, which can be visualized by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) observations (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The characteristic XRD reflections of Bi almost disappears when it reaches 1000, and even 1100&nbsp;°C, which can be well supported by the TEM inspections (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a,b</a>, Supporting Information), and no obvious crystallization peak of Bi can be detected at 1200&nbsp;°C, indicating that Bi is wholly removed at such temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0001">1e,f</a>). To well support this, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of CP-PVP film, CP-PVP-Bi-1.2 film, and Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O were comparatively performed, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0002">2b</a>. Notably, the accelerated decomposition of polymer matrix and Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O is evident for CP-PVP-Bi-1.2 film, compared to other two cases, and above 1200&nbsp;°C, no any mass loss can be found. Over the annealing process, the decomposition and dehydration process of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O will happen, which is decomposed into the basic salt of (BiO)<sub>2</sub>(NO)<sub>11</sub>(OH)·6H<sub>2</sub>O, then forms [BiO](NO)<sub>6</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O, and finally completely removes water and nitrogen to become <i>α</i>-Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0029_R_d435183099e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chinese J. Catal.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nitrogen oxide produced by the decomposition of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O can be captured by CP, and consumes the excess hydrogen in CP, fundamentally changing the carbonization process and inhibiting its graphitization, which plays a significant role in the formation of flexible film. Meanwhile, the generated <i>α</i>-Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is synchronously reduced to metallic bismuth by hydrogen and hydrocarbons. And the as-formed Bi NPs sublimate at high temperatures (1200 and 1300&nbsp;°C) to form pore and/or vesicle structures located in the interconnected HCNFs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0001">1e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information). Further HRTEM observations evidence the small lattice spacing of 0.335&nbsp;nm for the film achieved at 1300&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b,c</a>, Supporting Information). As we all know, with the increase in calcination temperature, due to the π–π interaction between polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons, the degree of graphitization will increase, along with the decreased degree of disorder and defect, which is not conducive to Na<sup>+</sup> storge of the film electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0030_R_d435183099e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the appropriate interlayer spacing (&gt;0.37&nbsp;nm) nm for efficient sodium storage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0027_R_d435183099e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the calcination temperature of 1200&nbsp;°C is optimized here for elegant fabrication of flexible HCNFs film as the&nbsp;anodes toward high-performance&nbsp;SDIBs.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19daae46-415b-4442-b2a4-02e0d026eace/adfm202414761-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/19daae46-415b-4442-b2a4-02e0d026eace/adfm202414761-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8e7512b1-0abe-44d2-9257-12b9280fb37e/adfm202414761-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19daae46-415b-4442-b2a4-02e0d026eace/adfm202414761-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of samples obtained by annealed at different temperatures as indicated, and b) TGA plots of CP-PVP-Bi-1.2 film, CP-PVP film, and Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O specimen.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The feasibility of other different metal nitrates including Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, and Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O utilized for the flexible HCNFs films was comprehensively investigated, as collected in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0003">3</a>. More interestingly, when the annealing temperature is set as 1000&nbsp;°C, no any material residue can be found in the quartz tube for all the nitrates just with an exception of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O. To get more in-depth understanding the significant role of the nitrates in the construction of flexible HCNFs films, an annealing temperature of 800&nbsp;°C was thus applied. Apparently, among the obtained specimens (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0003">3a–e</a>), only the fiber film containing Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O has the lowest shrinkage rate and the highest char yield, and displays the best-inherited flexible film feature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0003">3a</a>). By contrast, all other films are highly brittle and fragile (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0003">3b–e</a>). Considering the difference between of these nitrates, it is easy to conclude that the intrinsic characteristics of elemental Bi itself play a great role in the formation of flexible films. As is well established, from the perspective of spinnability, the electrostatic repulsive force in the spinning process with large electrical conductivity is exhausted, and it is difficult to form a Taylor cone.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0031_R_d435183099e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Rev. Method. Prim">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As regards the same ratio of metal nitrates, Bi has the worst electrical conductivity, which makes the case with Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O more easily form a fiber film than other cases. With comparative examination from FESEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0003">3f–o</a>) and TEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0003">3p–t</a>) images, it can be intuitively seen that the carbothermal reduction formed metal oxide NPs are seriously agglomerated in carbon fibers,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0032_R_d435183099e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Storage Mater">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> resulting in the collapse of fiber structures with low char yield. Because Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O has a wide thermal decomposition temperature range (60–800&nbsp;°C), while other metal nitrates have a narrow decomposition temperature (Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O: 32–210&nbsp;°C; Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O: 87–238&nbsp;°C; Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O: 30–310&nbsp;°C; Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O: 35–365&nbsp;°C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0033_R_d435183099e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Fuel">33</a>-<a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0035_R_d435183099e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Catal. Today">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, there is a strong interaction between the pyrolytic process of metal nitrates (just except Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O) with the same content and the carbon fiber, resulting in the collapse of the shrinkage structure of fibers. While, for the case with Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, the gradual sublimation feature of the in situ formed Bi NPs guarantees the structural stability of the fiber films, along with the construction of many pore and/or vesicles meanwhile. All in all, under the same conditions, the Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O is the best choice in terms of spinnability, structural stability, and char yield, effectively ensuring high flexibility of the&nbsp;as-achieved fiber films.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d10f9aef-15d7-47c5-b3b0-c3362194c278/adfm202414761-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d10f9aef-15d7-47c5-b3b0-c3362194c278/adfm202414761-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/71fff4e6-56e8-46b5-8997-05c093b1fc5a/adfm202414761-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d10f9aef-15d7-47c5-b3b0-c3362194c278/adfm202414761-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–e) Digital photos, f–o) FESEM, and p–t) TEM images of samples obtained by calcinating the eletrospun films with different nitrates at 800&nbsp;°C: (a,f,k,p) Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, (b,g,l,q) Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, (c,h,m,r) Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, (d,i,n,s) Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, and (e,j,o,t) Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414761-sec-0050-title">2.3 Fine Regulation of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O on Micro-Structures of Flexible HCNFs Films</h3>
            
            <p>In order to shed light upon the effects of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O on the micro-structures of HCNFs, various contents (0.0, 1.0, 1.2, 1.6, 2, and 2.4&nbsp;g) of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O in the precursor were purposefully adjusted. Clearly, the as-fabricated CNFs film without Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O shows relatively poor flexibility (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information), when compared with other cases with Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b–e</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the surface of these HCNFs with different Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>∙5H<sub>2</sub>O contents is rough and has a small amount of random bulges, especially when the content of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>∙5H<sub>2</sub>O is more than 1.2&nbsp;g (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0001">1c,d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7f–o</a>, Supporting Information). It is particularly mentioned that the serious fusion occurs for the case of CNFs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7f,k</a>, Supporting Information), which highlights that the introduction of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O is the key to the successful non-melting carbonization of fibers. As characterized by TEM, many pores in HCNFs are evenly distributed, along with a small amount of carbon vesicles located upon their surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0001">1e</a> and <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0004">4a–d</a>), while no well-discerned pores and/or vesicles are observed in as-resulted CNFs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), which explicitly authenticates the effective regulation of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O on the pore structure of fibers. As visualized, the number of pores gradually increase with the increase in contents of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O. Moreover, when the usage of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O reaches 2.0&nbsp;g (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0004">4c</a>), the surface of fibers is no longer smooth and structural collapse occurs, which turns out to be more distinct for the case with 2.4&nbsp;g of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0004">4d</a>). The phenomenon features that excessive Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O will produce a large amount of gas, which seriously destroys the fiber structure and produces a large number of defects. Such unique porous architecture of the HCNFs can be validated well by the N<sub>2</sub> sorption isotherms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information). The combination of Type I and type IV isotherms form an H<sub>3</sub>-type hysteresis loop, revealing the hierarchical micro-/meso-porous structure of the HCNFs films.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0023_R_d435183099e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Carbon">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific surface area (SSA) values of CNFs, HCNFs-1.0, HCNFs-1.2, HCNFs-1.6, HCNFs-2.0, and HCNFs-2.4 are estimated as 12.0, 17.8, 30.7, 29.6, 32.5, and 62.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The increase in SSA with more Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O indeed results from the abundant pores (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information), owing to the sublimation of elemental Bi during carbonization, which is consistent with the FESEM and TEM analysis above. It is particularly worthy of mentioning that the formed ample channels/space will favor the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and mitigate volume expansion meanwhile, enhancing both rate performance and cycling stability. However, the larger SSA will induce more electrolyte consumption in the initial charge and discharge process, and irreversible capture of sodium ions, resulting in low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0036_R_d435183099e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Storage Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e726bd8f-c7ae-4686-8f62-b6b0065a6b19/adfm202414761-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e726bd8f-c7ae-4686-8f62-b6b0065a6b19/adfm202414761-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/37a15583-5f4b-4cff-9227-10314ffe51ce/adfm202414761-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e726bd8f-c7ae-4686-8f62-b6b0065a6b19/adfm202414761-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–h) TEM and i–l) HRTEM images, and corresponding intensity profiles (the insets) for measuring interlayer spacings: (a,e,i) HCNFs-1.0, (b,f,j) HCNFs-1.6, (c,g,k) HCNFs-2.0, and (d,h,l) HCNFs-2.4.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition, the graphitic interlayer spacings of the as-obtained HCNFs are influenced hugely by the contents of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, as manifested by the TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0004">4e–h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information) and corresponding intensity profiles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0004">4i–l</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information). The interlayer spacings of CNFs, HCNFs-1.0, HCNFs-1.2, HCNFs-1.6, HCNFs-2.0, and HCNFs-2.4 are estimated as ≈0.365, ≈0.373, ≈0.419, ≈0.410, ≈0.412, and ≈0.414&nbsp;nm, respectively. Notably, the interlayer spacing can be expanded effectively with the addition of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, but it retains ≈0.4&nbsp;nm when Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O ≥1.2&nbsp;g, indicating that Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O reaches the saturation state for the HCNFs-1.2. Typically, the oxidative gases (O<sub>2</sub>, NO<sub>2</sub>, NO, etc.) released by the Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O during high-temperature calcination can continuously consume hydrogen, and these oxidation gases and solid products can also introduce cross-linking interaction at the same time, which effectively inhibits the rearrangement of carbon layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0037_R_d435183099e1317" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More disordered structure means larger interlayer spacing and more sodium storage sites, which is also the key to enhance the sodium-storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0030_R_d435183099e1323" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Phase structure of the films was further analyzed by XRD and Raman spectroscopy. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0005">5a</a>, all XRD patterns demonstrate two wide reflections at 2<i>θ</i> = 25° and 43°, corresponding to the typical (002) and (100) crystal planes of disordered carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0038_R_d435183099e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Pure Appl. Chem.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Clearly, a reflection peak at 2<i>θ</i> = ≈28° is discerned, corresponding to the characteristic peak of SiO<sub>2</sub> (PDF#14-6054), indicative of the inorganic impurities in the untreated CNFs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0039_R_d435183099e1348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Alloy. Compd.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typically, the crystal phases represented by different peaks can be divided into three types:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0040_R_d435183099e1353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano-Micro Lett.">40</a>-<a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0042_R_d435183099e1353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> I) The case of <i>d</i> spacing &gt;0.40&nbsp;nm, which corresponds to highly disordered microcrystals, whose Na<sup>+</sup> insertion energy barrier is close to 0&nbsp;eV, thereby facilitating unrestricted migration of Na<sup>+</sup> between layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0043_R_d435183099e1365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Lett.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> II) The <i>d</i> spacing is located in the range of 0.36–0.40&nbsp;nm, related to the relatively ordered, typical amorphous carbon, which always favors efficient Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0044_R_d435183099e1375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ChemElectroChem">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0045_R_d435183099e1375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Energy Storage Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> III) The one with a <i>d</i> spacing of &lt;0.36&nbsp;nm, which is too small to be accessible for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. In order to get more information about the microcrystalline phase, the (002) diffraction peaks were rationally fitted,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0046_R_d435183099e1385" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Storage Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and divided into two structures, i.e., HC region and soft carbon region, as manifested in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0005">5b</a>. According to Braggʼs law, the diffraction peak at 2<i>θ</i> = ≈22.1° indicates a layer distance of 0.403&nbsp;nm, and the diffraction peak at 2<i>θ</i> = ≈25.5° indicates a layer distance of 0.349&nbsp;nm. Obviously, the former belongs to the HC region, while the latter is for the soft carbon region. According to the fitting results, the HC domains for CNFs, HCNFs-1.0, HCNFs-1.2, HCNFs-1.6, HCNFs-2.0, and HCNFs-2.4 are achieved as ≈45%, ≈48%, ≈60%, ≈61%, ≈63%, and ≈64%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0005">5c</a>). Such result confirms that the disorder degree is gradually enhanced with increasing the Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O proportion, which are consistent with the aforementioned HRTEM analysis. Raman tests were performed to better understand the lattice defects and graphitization degree of HCNFs. The two strong peaks at 1349 and 1599 cm<sup>‒1</sup>, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0005">5d</a>, correspond to the characteristic D-band and G-band, respectively. The former indicates the degree of defect or disorder, and the latter reflects the in-plane tensile vibration of sp<sup>2</sup> hybrid structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0047_R_d435183099e1415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mater. Sci. Int. J. Biol. Macromol.">47</a>, <a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0048_R_d435183099e1415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Carbon">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Accordingly, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> integral peak area ratio is used to evaluate the graphitization degree in carbon materials. The larger the value of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub>, the more defects, and the lower the graphitization degree. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio gradually rises from 0.87 (CNFs) to 0.93 (HCNFs-2.4), indicating the more disordered structure and/or more defects with the increase in Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O content (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0005">5d</a>). Although it will provide sufficient accessible sites and diffusion paths for the rapid diffusion of sodium ions, however, too many defects will always lead to low ICE due to the irreversible capture of sodium ions by defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0036_R_d435183099e1446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Storage Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6f32b529-2c34-4df7-85e5-ce1fd55a7d29/adfm202414761-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6f32b529-2c34-4df7-85e5-ce1fd55a7d29/adfm202414761-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/927ccbf5-00ce-4d72-9c9a-b159bad39c7b/adfm202414761-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6f32b529-2c34-4df7-85e5-ce1fd55a7d29/adfm202414761-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of CNFs and HCNFs samples. b) Schematic diagram for carbon crystal structure. c) Peak fitting files of the (002) peaks for CNFs and HCNFs samples. d) Raman spectra and fitted curves of CNFs and HCNFs samples as indicated.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The influence of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O contents upon the functional groups and elemental composition of the films was investigated by employing Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) techniques. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0006">6a</a> displays FT-IR spectra of CNFs and HCNFs, and detailed band assignments are collected (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The band at 3431 cm<sup>‒1</sup> is related to the stretching vibration of hydroxyl (─OH) and carbonyl (C═O) groups in fatty chains.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0049_R_d435183099e1491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Fuel">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensity of such band gradually amplifies with an increase in Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, suggesting a rise in oxygen-containing functional groups, which is another strong evidence for the close interaction of CP with Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O (and/or other nitrogen oxides produced by its decomposition). And the presence of a band at 630 cm<sup>‒1</sup> (C═N) indicates the transfer of N atoms from NO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and/or PVP to the carbon framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0037_R_d435183099e1515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the gradual weakening of the band at 1120 cm<sup>‒1</sup> (fatty chain CH<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and CH<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>) confirms a decrease in hydrogen consumption, which fully verifies that the high-temperature decomposition of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O can continuously consume hydrogen, thereby suppressing carbon rearrangement.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7dd56aa2-a45c-4f46-bdbd-ddfdca7ac7b7/adfm202414761-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7dd56aa2-a45c-4f46-bdbd-ddfdca7ac7b7/adfm202414761-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c58fe8a5-ebe9-405f-ae1a-0a1656999697/adfm202414761-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7dd56aa2-a45c-4f46-bdbd-ddfdca7ac7b7/adfm202414761-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) FT-IR spectra of CNFs and HCNFs. b) Specific atomic ratios of C, O, and N in the six products. c) High-resolution of C 1s (the left), O 1s (the middle), and N 1s (the right) XPS spectra of CNFs and HCNFs as indicated.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As evidenced by the XPS survey spectra of HCNFs, all samples are predominantly composed of three elements, namely C, N, and O (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). According to the fitting results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0006">6b</a>), oxygen and nitrogen contents have the same trend with the increase of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O content, both of which increase first and then decrease. As noted, the oxygen (≈2.4 at.%) and nitrogen (≈1.1 at.%) contents of HCNFs-1.2 are the highest among all products. We believe that the reason for the phenomenon lies upon that the negatively charged surface of CP will inhibit the adsorption of nitrate anion, so excessive Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O (&gt;1.2&nbsp;g) reacts violently during the pyrolysis process, and the nitrate anion cannot fully interact with CP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0049_R_d435183099e1576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Fuel">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-resolution C 1s, O 1s and N 1s spectra of the six films are comparatively depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0006">6c</a>. The C 1s spectrum exhibit four distinct peaks at banding energies of 284.7, 285.3, 286.3, and 289.3&nbsp;eV, indicating the presence of C─C, C─N, C─O, and C═O bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0050_R_d435183099e1585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Environments">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The well-defined C─O (286.3&nbsp;eV) and C═O (289.3&nbsp;eV) peaks should be related to the interaction between Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O (and/or its decomposition products) and CP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0051_R_d435183099e1597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O 1s spectra show three recognizable peaks for the oxygen functional groups including C═O (531&nbsp;eV), C-O (532.3&nbsp;eV), and COOH (533.5&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0052_R_d435183099e1602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, the C═O can store/release Na<sup>+</sup> through the reversible reaction “C═O + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup>↔ C─O-Na”.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0053_R_d435183099e1614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Batteries Supercaps">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Clearly, the content of active C = O initially increases, and then decreases, and the highest content of ≈0.71 at.% is achieved by HCNFs-1.2 (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the N 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0006">6c</a>) depict three fitted peaks at 398.1, 401.3, and 402.3&nbsp;eV, which can be attributed to pyridinic nitrogen (N-6), pyrrolic nitrogen (N-5), and graphitic nitrogen (N-Q), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0037_R_d435183099e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nitrogen species located in the carbon skeleton is probably introduced through PVP pyrolysis and nitrogen oxides (such as NO<sub>2</sub> and NO) released by Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O. Especially, N-6 and N-5 are sp<sup>2</sup> hybrid bonds to the C atom on the ring, which can provide more active sites, enhance the surface-induced capacitive process, and facilitate the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0054_R_d435183099e1644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Energy Environ. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both N-6 and N-5, as compared with other nitrogen groups (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), reach their maximum values (≈0.35 and ≈0.55 at.%) for the case of HCNFs-1.2 as well. According to comprehensive analysis above, the existence of these active hetero-atomic groups will improve the surface polarity and wettability of electrodes and increase electroactive sites, which benefit for the improvement in sodium-storage properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0055_R_d435183099e1653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To sum up, the as-fabricated porous HCNFs flexible films are endowed with distinctive merits toward efficient sodium storage as competitive self-standing anodes. First, the self-supporting characteristic eliminates the use of conductive agents, polymeric binders, and current collectors, which significantly improves the conductivity/stability of electrodes, and cell-level energy density. Second, 3D porous architecture provides abundant and open channels for rapid diffusion of sodium ions, meeting the urgent need for more Na<sup>+</sup> particularly at high rates. Third, the abundant active N-/O-surface groups increase electroactive sites, and significantly improve the surface wettability and conductivity of the electrode material. Last but not least, the highly disordered graphitic microcrystalline zones of larger interlayer spacings afford more electroactive sodium-storage sites, and facilitate the rapid insertion/extraction of sodium ion, enhancing the platform-region capacity contribution even under large current densities.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414761-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Evaluation of Flexible HCNFs Films</h3>
            
            <p>Thanks to their striking micro-structural and componential merits, the achieved flexible HCNFs films hold enormous promise in sodium storage as a self-supporting anode for SDIBs. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7a</a> illustrates the initial three cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of HCNFs-1.2 at a sweep rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup> within a voltage range of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). In the first cathodic scanning, two irreversible reduction peaks emerge at ≈0.5 and 1.1&nbsp;V, but then disappears in subsequent scanning cycles, which is ascribed to the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and irreversible trapping of Na<sup>+</sup> by high-energy defect sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0018_R_d435183099e1684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The well-overlapped CV curves with a pair of oxidation-reduction peaks at low potentials over the subsequent cycles are evident, indicative of the excellent reversibility in sodium ion storage within the carbonaceous matrix. The galvanostatic charge and discharge (GCD) was tested (0.1 A g<sup>‒1</sup>) along with the first three-cycle plots (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Interestingly, the GCD plots of CNFs only have slope region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting its unique storage mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on the film surface. However, the GCD profiles of HCNFs electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b–f</a>, Supporting Information) are composed of the high-potential slope region and the low-potential platform region, which correspond to the (de)adsorption on the surface/edge/defects, the (de)insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the interlayers, and nanopore filling, which confirms the mixed sodium-storage behaviors occurring in these HCNFs electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0027_R_d435183099e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Obviously, the HCNFs-1.2 anode shows a reversible sodium storage capacity of 216.3 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> after the three cycles, even larger than those of CNFs (96.5 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>), HCNFs-1.0 (206.3 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>), HCNFs-1.6 (209.2 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>), HCNFs-2.0 (166.8 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>), and HCNFs-2.4 (159.2 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>), for which the remarkable dominant contrition of ≈54% from the low-voltage region should be well responsible (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, it is clearly found that compared to the CNFs anode, all the ICE values of the resultant HCNFs are greatly enhanced by ≈20 to 30% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), along with an ICE as high as ≈80.1% for HCNFs-1.2. This highlights that the addition of suitable Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O can effectively control surface area/defects and functional groups, thus limiting the formation of SEI film and the irreversible embedding of sodium ions in the matrix material.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8b7e2054-aa0f-44bc-9768-4b25966f0ead/adfm202414761-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8b7e2054-aa0f-44bc-9768-4b25966f0ead/adfm202414761-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c6764cb1-87b3-418b-a7fb-3d64dec0d861/adfm202414761-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8b7e2054-aa0f-44bc-9768-4b25966f0ead/adfm202414761-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of the CNFs and HCNFs film anodes: a) CV profiles of HCNFs-1.2 at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup>. b) Rate properties (0.05–5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>). c) Cyclic stability at 0.1A g<sup>‒1</sup>. d) Calculated sodium diffusion coefficients during (de)adsorption processes. e) CV curves at different scan rates, f) Logarithmic relationship between peak current and scan rate, g) pseudocapacitive contribution (the red) and battery-type contribution (the blue) at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup> along with (h) summary of specific contributions of the two at different scan rates, and i) cyclic stability (1.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>) of the HCNFs-1.2 film anode. The inset in panel (i) for the data in the first twenty cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Based on the above analysis, the mechanism of sodium ion storage of CNFs and HCNFS-1.2 can be summarized by clarifying the relationship between the microstructure and electrochemical properties of HCNFs. Owing to the high degree of graphitization exhibited by CNFs and their well-ordered structure, Na<sup>+</sup> ions are readily absorbed at defect sites on the active surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>, Supporting Information), with only a limited number of ions penetrating into the graphite layer. The comprehensive regulation of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O makes HCNFs-1.2 have porous structure and a large number of O/N-based functional groups, which provides more adsorption sites for Na<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the large layer spacing (≈0.419&nbsp;nm), abundant nanopores provide more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> (de)insertion. Therefore, the sodium storage mechanism of HCNFs-1.2 is characterized by an absorption-insertion-pore filling model (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, Supporting Information). More specifically, at ≈1.1–0.3&nbsp;V (green region), Na<sup>+</sup> is adsorbed on the sur-/interfaces and/or defect. In the 0.3–0.1&nbsp;V voltage range (yellow region), a lower tilt region can be seen, which may be caused by sodium ions inserted into the graphite microcrystalline zone. The platform region less than 0.1&nbsp;V (purple part) is ascribed to the pore filling process.</p>
            
            <p>The rate performance of the anodes was investigated within the current density range of 0.05–5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. The HCNFs-1.2 film, as plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7b</a>, presents large reversible capacities of ≈238.4, ≈188.9, ≈141.3, ≈126.7, ≈112.2, ≈98.8, and ≈75.6 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>, at all current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0, respectively, corresponding to a small capacity degradation of ≈68.1% with a 100-fold increase in current density. By contrast, other CNFs, HCNFs-1.0, HCNFs-1.6, HCNFs-2.0 and HCNFs-2.4 film anodes just can achieve reversible capacities of ≈167.6/≈30.5, ≈191.7/≈39.8, ≈207.1/≈35.5, ≈208.9/≈43.1, and ≈142.2/≈18.3 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at current densities of 0.05/5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively. More encouragingly, when the current density is restored to 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, a large capacity of ≈223.8 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> still can be restored for HCNFs-1.2. The superb high-rate behaviors of HCNFs-1.2 is reasonably attributed to its strong capability of rapid Na<sup>+</sup> (de)insertion even at a high rate of 5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, as evidenced by its well-discerned low-voltage platform region (&lt;0.1&nbsp;V) when compared to CNFs and other HCNFs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Over 300 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>‒1</sup> (after five-cycle pre-activation at 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>), as collected in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7c</a>, HCNFs-1.2 demonstrates a modest capacity decay of ≈17.7% over cycling, even better than those of CNFs (≈40.5%), HCNFs-1 (≈21.7%), HCNFs-1.6 (≈37.1%), HCNFs-2.0 (≈23.8%) and HCNFs-2.4 (≈44.1%). The noticeable storage capacity decay with the increase in Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O content here should result from the fact that excessive Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O seriously damages the fibers/film structure, and renders more defects and larger accessible area for irreversible capture of sodium ions, along with the decreased active functional sites and layer spacings, which leads to the decline in reversible capacities, rate behaviors and cycle performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7b,c</a>).</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To better unveil the electrochemical sodium-storage kinetic of these films, GITT measurement was conducted at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>‒1</sup> along with a relaxation interval (<i>τ</i>) of 1800 s (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Obviously, HCNFs-1.2 always shows a higher <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1867b3e9-d0f0-4c9d-ac83-51c273ac42a7/adfm202414761-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202414761:adfm202414761-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202414761-math-0003.png">

                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> value (≈1E<sup>‒8.5</sup>–1E<sup>‒8</sup>) than other counterparts, regardless of the charging or discharging process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7d</a>), which guarantees the fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate, especially for efficient sodium storage at high current densities. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7e</a> demonstrates CV curves of HCNFs-1.2 at different scanning rates from 0.1 to 5.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup>. Accordingly, a relationship between CV peak current (<i>i</i>) and scanning rate (<i>v</i>) is described as: <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup>b</sup>, in which both of <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are variable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0026_R_d435183099e1995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, the <i>b</i> value can be determined by plotting the slope of the log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>) plots. Specifically, the <i>b</i> value of approaching 0.5 indicates that diffusion-dominated Na<sup>+</sup>-storage process (i.e., typical battery behavior), while, for the <i>b</i> value of 1.0, the pseudo-capacitance-dominated charge storage process is well established.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0057_R_d435183099e2013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After linear fitting, the <i>b</i> values of HCNFs-1.2 are estimated as 0.84 and 0.86 for the anodic and cathodic processes, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7f</a>). It means the synergistic sodium-storage characteristics of diffusion-controlled (de)intercalation and sur-/interface driven pseudo-capacitive processes for HCNFs-1.2, in which the latter dominates its charge storage behavior. Further, the mixing behavior can be quantified by using the equation: <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/56679adc-c8b5-4f35-9907-164ee0be1e8f/adfm202414761-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202414761:adfm202414761-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202414761-math-0004.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>

                        </msub>

                  </semantics></math>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0058_R_d435183099e2043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Energy Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> where <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i> are constants. The <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5fe8da68-d288-4e08-b1c0-6b8aa4fb3bdc/adfm202414761-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202414761:adfm202414761-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202414761-math-0005.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>k</mi>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                        </msub>

                           <mi>v</mi>
                        </msqrt>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{k}_{\mathrm{2}}}\sqrt v $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> correspond to the capacitive and diffusion-controlled contributions, respectively. The pseudo-capacitive contribution (the red zone) of HCNFs-1.2 at a scanning rate of 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup> is determined as 80.7% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7g</a>). Moreover, the high contributions can be observed for HCNFs-1.2 with an increase from 64.8% (0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup>) to 96.0% (5.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup>), as illustrated from the summary diagram of capacitance contribution to the total specific capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7h</a>). The remarkable <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/96469970-3b9a-4ece-8d37-0902c3350721/adfm202414761-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202414761:adfm202414761-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202414761-math-0006.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>N</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> and high capacitive contributions cooperatively account for the impressive high-rate capability of the HCNFs-1.2 film anode. Except for this, the excellent long-term cyclic performance of HCNFs-1.2, particularly under high current densities, is of enormous significance for its practical application. Remarkably, the film anode, after 10-cycle charging/discharging activation at 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, exhibits a capacity retention rate of 87.3% after 1000 uninterrupted charge–discharge cycles, corresponding to only 0.0127% capacity loss per cycle, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0007">7i</a>. It is particularly worthy of noting that the comprehensive electrochemical properties of HCNFs-1.2 are obviously better than other self-supporting carbon film electrodes, and pitch-derived carbon materials (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Such excellent electrochemical performance of HCNFs-1.2 can be reasonably attributed to its hierarchical porous structure and robust 3D interconnected network structure, which can accelerate the diffusion of sodium ions. Besides, the unique vesicle structure effectively solves the issue of structural collapse caused by the electrochemical (de)insertion of sodium ions especially under high rates, making it more commercially applied for SDIBs as an anode.</div>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414761-sec-0070-title">2.5 Electrochemical Properties of HCNFs-1.2//Graphite SDIBs</h3>
            
            <p>Clearly, the long sodium storage platform (&lt;0.1&nbsp;V) and high ICE (80.1%) of HCNFs-1.2 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a>, Supporting Information) can hugely improve the average operating voltage, and favor the enhanced CE and capacity of full devices, when assembled into SDIBs as an attractive anode. Furthermore, HCNFs-1.2 is endowed with striking rate behaviors, as well-discerned low-voltage platform even at a high rate of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a>, Supporting Information). All in all, the outstanding electrochemical performance of HCNFs-1.2 mentioned above render it highly suitable for application in SDIBs. Before cell assembly, the HCNFs-1.2 anode was electrochemically pre-sodiated (i.e., subjected to five charging-discharging cycles at 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>‒1</sup>) in half cells in order to compensate the Na<sup>+</sup> consumption over the SEI formation, enhancing the ICE of full SDIBs. Typically, the full device was constructed with pre-sodiated HCNFs-1.2 (the anode) | NaPF<sub>6</sub> (the electrolyte) | commercial graphite (the cathode), as schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0008">8a</a>. The mass ratio of HCNFs-1.2 to graphite is consistently maintained at 1: 3 for ensuring capacity matching between the two involved electrodes. As regards the HCNFs-1.2//graphite SDIBs, during charging, the Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> will move to HCNFs-1.2 anode and graphite cathode, respectively, and the two ions return to the electrolyte again in the discharge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0008">8a</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0008">8b</a> profiles the CV curves of graphite | NaPF<sub>6</sub> | Na and HCNFs-1.2 | NaPF<sub>6</sub> | Na half cells to investigate the insertion and extraction processes of graphite (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) and HCNFs-1.2 (Na<sup>+</sup>). Evidently, Na<sup>+</sup> is electrochemical embedded/removed from HCNFs-1.2 at low potentials, while PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> is embedded/removed from graphite at high potentials (commonly &gt;4.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0010_R_d435183099e2165" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The large gap between the insertion/removal potentials between the HCNFs-1.2 and graphite endows the SDIBs with a high operating voltage. Here, the electrochemical properties of SDIBs are evaluated in the voltage range of 1.0–5.0&nbsp;V.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414761-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b414826f-6cc6-48b2-a3f8-7ad2e330cb64/adfm202414761-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b414826f-6cc6-48b2-a3f8-7ad2e330cb64/adfm202414761-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/23534ff3-a67a-4978-9756-b1bdb9c5e9d6/adfm202414761-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b414826f-6cc6-48b2-a3f8-7ad2e330cb64/adfm202414761-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414761-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414761" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the HCNFs-1.2//Graphite SDIBs: a) Schematic illustration for operating mechanism of the device. b) CV curves of the HCNFs-1.2 anode and graphite cathode at 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate capability. d) Typical charge–discharge plots at different current densities. e) Comparison in specific capacities with other previously reported SDIBs. f) Long-duration cycling stability at 1.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0008">8c</a> depicts rate properties of the full devices over the current density range of 0.1–5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. Typically, after the five-cycle activation of SDIBs at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, our constructed HCNFs-1.2//graphite SDIBs shows striking rate behaviors with average discharge capacities of ≈90.9, ≈84.3, ≈72.4, ≈69.7, ≈54.8, and ≈47.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>‒1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, indicating an appealing capacity retention of ≈52.5%. In addition, the GCD plots of the device under different current densities are similar, and the charge–discharge platform just presents a slight shift (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0008">8d</a>), which reveals the small electrochemical polarization of our assembled SDIBs, meeting the stringent requirement of high-rate scenarios. More attractively, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0008">8e</a>, specific capacities of our SDIBs at different current densities are competitive with those of other reported counterparts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0007_R_d435183099e2216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0009_R_d435183099e2216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0059_R_d435183099e2216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Mater. Today">59</a>-<a href="#adfm202414761-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414761-bib-0064_R_d435183099e2216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The long-span cycling characteristics of HCNFs-1.2//graphite SDIBs at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> is exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414761-fig-0008">8f</a>. After 1500 continuous charge–discharge cycles, the capacity retention is estimated to be 78.4% (i.e., 0.0144% capacity loss per cycle), coupled with high CE values of ≈100% during cycling. Such encouraging electrochemical properties of SDIBs in terms of high-rate capacity and cycling stability authenticates their broad application prospect.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414761-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a simple yet efficient Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O-assisted electrospinning-carbonization strategy, to the best of our knowledge, was first developed in the contribution to successfully fabricate 3D porous flexible HCNFs film by using the commercial LSP CP as the low-cost precursor and the low-carbonization-rate PVP as spinning aid. Systematical experiments/characterizations revealed that the introduction of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O and the pre-oxidation process were of equal significance to the successful non-melting carbonization of CP fibers. More particularly, the Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O generated nitrogen oxide effectively consumed the excess hydrogen in CP, fundamentally changing the carbonization process and inhibiting its graphitization, which played a significant role in the formation of flexible HCNFs films. Meanwhile, the spontaneous sublimation of carbothermal reduction induced nano Bi led to the formation of pores and/or vesicles located in the HCNFs. Accordingly, the intrinsic formation mechanism of flexible HCNFs films was rationally put forward. Through elaborate adjustment in Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O content and calcination temperatures, the porosity, interlayer spacing, surface hetero-atomic modification, and graphite microcrystalline structure, etc., of the resulted flexible HCNFs films were effectively optimized. The optimal HCNFs-1.2 film, when evaluated as the self-supporting anodes for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, obtained large reversible specific capacities of 238.4/75.6 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at current densities of 0.05/2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, and a modest capacity degradation of 0.0127% per cycle after 1000 consecutive cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. More competitively, our constructed HCNFs-1.2//graphite SDIBs afforded a reversible capacity of ≈47.7 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at a high rate of 5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, along with a superb long-duration cycling stability with a capacity decay of 21.6% after 1500 uninterrupted charge–discharge cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, highlighting its promising application prospect. More significantly, our work provided an all-new perspective on design and synthesis of flexible HCNFs films with appealing sodium-storage capability as self-standing anodes for advanced SDIBs, and even other sodium-containing electrochemical energy storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414761-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202414761-sec-0120-title"> Synthesis of HCNFs</h4>
                  
                  <p>Typically, 1.0&nbsp;g of polyvinyl pyrrolidone (PVP, M<sub>w</sub> = 130 000) was dissolved to 10&nbsp;mL of a mixture of <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF), and N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) at a volumetric ratio of 1:1 under magnetic stirring. 1.0&nbsp;g of coal pitch (CP, softening point: 180&nbsp;°C; ash content: 15.9&nbsp;wt.%; H/C: 0.65) and 1.2&nbsp;g of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O to the above transparent solution. After stirred overnight, the resulted polymer solution was electrospun under the conditions of 30&nbsp;°C, the humidity &lt;25%, applied voltage of 20&nbsp;kV, a spinning speed of 0.7&nbsp;mL h<sup>‒1</sup>, and the distance of 20&nbsp;cm between the receiving aluminum foil and the needle. The electrospun nanofiber films were collected on the aluminum foil and dried in an oven at 60&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h (denoted as CP-PVP-Bi-1.2). After stabilized in a muffle furnace at 230&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h, the fiber precursor was heated from room temperature to 600&nbsp;°C at a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> under an argon atmosphere and held for 1&nbsp;h, and then annealed at 1200&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h. The CP-derived porous hard carbon nanofibers (HCNFs) flexible film was obtained finally, and denoted as HCNFs-1.2. The stabilization at 600&nbsp;°C effectively reduced the damage of fiber structure, and further improved mechanical properties of carbon fibers over high-temperature calcination. For comparison, other samples were also prepared with different amounts (0.0, 1.0, 1.6, 2.0, and 2.4&nbsp;g) of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, and denoted as CNFs, HCNFs-1.0, HCNFs-1.6, HCNFs-2.0, and HCNFs-2.4, respectively. For comparison, other samples were also prepared with different nitrates including Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, and Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O. When treated at 800&nbsp;°C, the fiber films were obtained, while other synthetic parameters were the same as those of HCNFs-1.2.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202414761-sec-0130-title"> Characterization of Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Detailed morphologies and micro-structures of products were mainly characterized by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, QUANTA FEG250), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), scanning TEM (STEM), and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM, JEOL JEM-2100 Plus). Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) and corresponding elemental analysis were performed using TEM X-ray spectrometers. X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) patterns were achieved by using Cu-K<i>α</i> radiation source (<i>λ</i> = 0.15 418&nbsp;nm, Rigaku Ultima IV X-ray diffractometer) with a scanning speed of 10 ° min<sup>‒1</sup> within a 2<i>θ</i> range of 5°–80°. Specific elemental compositions, bonding types, and other chemical structures of the samples were analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo ESCALAB 250XI X) using an Al-K<i>α</i> monochromatic source with energy of 1486.6&nbsp;eV. Raman spectroscopy (Laman, Lab RAM HR, 532&nbsp;nm laser) was used to analyze the changes of binding bonds in the samples. Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area analysis and pore size distribution data were detected using N<sub>2</sub> adsorption desorption isotherm method (NOVA 2000, Quantachrome). Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra were performed by using Nicolet iS50 spectrometer with a KBr pellet in the range from 400 to 4000 cm<sup>‒1</sup>. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, DTG-60H) was conducted in air with a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>‒1</sup> in the temperature range from 25 to 1100&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414761-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202414761-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The self-standing HCNFs anodes with a diameter of 12&nbsp;mm were obtained using a punch machine and the mass in each self-supporting electrode was ≈1.0&nbsp;mg. The metallic Na was used as the counter/reference electrode, along with the glass fiber filter (Whatman GF/F) and NaPF<sub>6</sub> (NP-030) dissolved in the ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate mixture (by 1:1 in volume ratio) as the separator and electrolyte, respectively. As for fabrication of the graphite cathode, poly(vinylidene fluoride), acetylene black and commercial graphite (1:2:7 in weight ratio) in the NMP were mixed, and coated on a fresh Al foil. After dried in vacuum at a temperature of 110&nbsp;°C, the graphite cathode was used to fabricate half-cell configurations and/or full devices. Before assembling the full cells, the HCNFs anodes were electrochemical cycled for five times in a half cell at 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>. The weight ratio of the cathode to anode was designed as 3.0 for charge balance. The total mass load of the two electrodes was ≈4.0&nbsp;mg, which was used to calculate the capacity and current density of the SIDBs. All the electrochemical properties were evaluated by coin cells (CR2032) assembled in an Ar-filled glove box (MBRAUN UNIlab Plus, both O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O ≤0.1). Typical charge–discharge measurements were conducted on a Land CT2001A battery tester, along with cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves recorded with an electrochemical workstation (Ivium).</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
